But Tometz addressed a challenge to a statute requiring race-consciousness in drawing school attendance boundaries\xe2\x80\x94an issue well beyond the scope of the question presented in these cases. Importantly, it considered that issue only under rational-basis review, 39 Ill. 2d, at 600, 237 N.\xc2\xa0E. 2d, at 502 (\xe2\x80\x9cThe test of any legislative classification essentially is one of reasonableness\xe2\x80\x9d), which even the dissent grudgingly recognizes is an improper standard for evaluating express racial classifications. Other cases cited are similarly inapplicable. See, e.g., Citizens for Better Ed. v. Goose Creek Consol. Independent School Dist., 719 S.\xc2\xa0W. 2d 350, 352\xe2\x80\x93353 (Tex. App. 1986) (upholding rezoning plan under rational-basis review).[Footnote 16]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Breyer\xe2\x80\x99s dissent next looks for authority to a footnote in Washington v. Seattle School Dist. No. 1, 458 U. S. 457, 472, n.\xc2\xa015 (1982), post, at 56\xe2\x80\x9357, but there this Court expressly noted that it was not passing on the propriety of race-conscious student assignments in the absence of a finding of de jure segregation. Similarly, the citation of Crawford v. Board of Ed. of Los Angeles, 458 U. S. 527 (1982), post, at 24, in which a state referendum prohibiting a race-based assignment plan was challenged, is inapposite\xe2\x80\x94in Crawford the Court again expressly reserved the question presented by these cases. 458 U. S., at 535, n.\xc2\xa011. Such reservations and preliminary analyses of course did not decide the merits of this question\xe2\x80\x94as evidenced by the disagreement among the lower courts on this issue. Compare Eisenberg, 197 F.\xc2\xa03d, at 133, with Comfort, 418 F.\xc2\xa03d, at 13.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Breyer\xe2\x80\x99s dissent also asserts that these cases are controlled by Grutter, claiming that the existence of a compelling interest in these cases \xe2\x80\x9cfollows a fortiori\xe2\x80\x9d from Grutter, post, at 41, 64\xe2\x80\x9366, and accusing us of tacitly overruling that case, see post, at 64\xe2\x80\x9366. The dissent overreads Grutter, however, in suggesting that it renders pure racial balancing a constitutionally compelling interest; Grutter itself recognized that using race simply to achieve racial balance would be \xe2\x80\x9cpatently unconstitutional,\xe2\x80\x9d 539 U. S., at 330. The Court was exceedingly careful in describing the interest furthered in Grutter as \xe2\x80\x9cnot an interest in simple ethnic diversity\xe2\x80\x9d but rather a \xe2\x80\x9cfar broader array of qualifications and characteristics\xe2\x80\x9d in which race was but a single element. 539 U. S., at 324\xe2\x80\x93325 (internal quotation marks omitted). We take the Grutter Court at its word. We simply do not understand how Justice Breyer can maintain that classifying every schoolchild as black or white, and using that classification as a determinative factor in assigning children to achieve pure racial balance, can be regarded as \xe2\x80\x9cless burdensome, and hence more narrowly tailored\xe2\x80\x9d than the consideration of race in Grutter, post, at 47, when the Court in Grutter stated that \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he importance of \xe2\x80\xa6 individualized consideration\xe2\x80\x9d in the program was \xe2\x80\x9cparamount,\xe2\x80\x9d and consideration of race was one factor in a \xe2\x80\x9chighly individualized, holistic review.\xe2\x80\x9d 539 U. S., at 337. Certainly if the constitutionality of the stark use of race in these cases were as established as the dissent would have it, there would have been no need for the extensive analysis undertaken in Grutter. In light of the foregoing, Justice Breyer\xe2\x80\x99s appeal to stare decisis rings particularly hollow. See post, at 65\xe2\x80\x9366.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0At the same time it relies on inapplicable desegregation cases, misstatements of admitted dicta, and other noncontrolling pronouncements, Justice Breyer\xe2\x80\x99s dissent candidly dismisses the significance of this Court\xe2\x80\x99s repeated holdings that all racial classifications must be reviewed under strict scrutiny, see post, at 31\xe2\x80\x9333, 35\xe2\x80\x9336, arguing that a different standard of review should be applied because the districts use race for beneficent rather than malicious purposes, see post, at 31\xe2\x80\x9336.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0This Court has recently reiterated, however, that \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98all racial classifications [imposed by government] \xe2\x80\xa6 must be analyzed by a reviewing court under strict scrutiny.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Johnson, 543 U. S., at 505 (quoting Adarand, 515 U. S., at 227; emphasis added by Johnson Court). See also Grutter, supra, at 326 (\xe2\x80\x9c[G]overnmental action based on race\xe2\x80\x94a group classification long recognized as in most circumstances irrelevant and therefore prohibited\xe2\x80\x94should be subjected to detailed judicial inquiry\xe2\x80\x9d (internal quotation marks and emphasis omitted)). Justice Breyer nonetheless relies on the good intentions and motives of the school districts, stating that he has found \xe2\x80\x9cno case that \xe2\x80\xa6 repudiated this constitutional asymmetry between that which seeks to exclude and that which seeks to include members of minority races.\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 29 (emphasis in original). We have found many. Our cases clearly reject the argument that motives affect the strict scrutiny analysis. See Johnson, supra, at 505 (\xe2\x80\x9cWe have insisted on strict scrutiny in every context, even for so-called \xe2\x80\x98benign\xe2\x80\x99 racial classifications\xe2\x80\x9d); Adarand, 515 U. S., at 227 (rejecting idea that \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98benign\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d racial classifications may be held to \xe2\x80\x9cdifferent standard\xe2\x80\x9d); Croson, 488 U. S., at 500 (\xe2\x80\x9cRacial classifications are suspect, and that means that simple legislative assurances of good intention cannot suffice\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0This argument that different rules should govern racial classifications designed to include rather than exclude is not new; it has been repeatedly pressed in the past, see, e.g., Gratz, 539 U. S., at 282 (Breyer, J., concurring in judgment); id., at 301 (Ginsburg, J., dissenting); Adarand, supra, at 243 (Stevens, J., dissenting); Wygant, 476 U. S., at 316\xe2\x80\x93317 (Stevens, J., dissenting), and has been repeatedly rejected. See also Bakke, 438 U. S., at 289\xe2\x80\x93291 (opinion of Powell, J.) (rejecting argument that strict scrutiny should be applied only to classifications that disadvantage minorities, stating \xe2\x80\x9c[r]acial and ethnic distinctions of any sort are inherently suspect and thus call for the most exacting judicial examination\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The reasons for rejecting a motives test for racial classifications are clear enough. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Court\xe2\x80\x99s emphasis on \xe2\x80\x98benign racial classifications\xe2\x80\x99 suggests confidence in its ability to distinguish good from harmful governmental uses of racial criteria. History should teach greater humility\xe2\x80\xa6\xc2\xa0. \xe2\x80\x98[B]enign\xe2\x80\x99 carries with it no independent meaning, but reflects only acceptance of the current generation\xe2\x80\x99s conclusion that a politically acceptable burden, imposed on particular citizens on the basis of race, is reasonable.\xe2\x80\x9d Metro Broadcasting, 497 U. S., at 609\xe2\x80\x93610 (O\xe2\x80\x99Connor, J., dissenting). See also Adarand, supra, at 226 (\xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98[I]t may not always be clear that a so-called preference is in fact benign\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d (quoting Bakke, supra, at 298 (opinion of Powell, J.))). Accepting Justice Breyer\xe2\x80\x99s approach would \xe2\x80\x9cdo no more than move us from \xe2\x80\x98separate but equal\xe2\x80\x99 to \xe2\x80\x98unequal but benign.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Metro Broadcasting, supra, at 638 (Kennedy, J., dissenting).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Breyer speaks of bringing \xe2\x80\x9cthe races\xe2\x80\x9d together (putting aside the purely black-and-white nature of the plans), as the justification for excluding individuals on the basis of their race. See post, at 28\xe2\x80\x9329. Again, this approach to racial classifications is fundamentally at odds with our precedent, which makes clear that the Equal Protection Clause \xe2\x80\x9cprotect[s] persons, not groups,\xe2\x80\x9d Adarand, 515 U. S., at 227 (emphasis in original). See ibid. (\xe2\x80\x9c[A]ll governmental action based on race\xe2\x80\x94a group classification long recognized as \xe2\x80\x98in most circumstances irrelevant and therefore prohibited,\xe2\x80\x99 Hirabayashi [v. United States, 320 U. S. 81, 100 (1943)]\xe2\x80\x94should be subjected to detailed judicial inquiry to ensure that the personal right to equal protection of the laws has not been infringed\xe2\x80\x9d (first emphasis in original); Metro Broadcasting, supra, at 636 (\xe2\x80\x9c[O]ur Constitution protects each citizen as an individual, not as a member of a group\xe2\x80\x9d (Kennedy, J., dissenting)); Bakke, supra, at 289 (opinion of Powell, J.) (Fourteenth Amendment creates rights \xe2\x80\x9cguaranteed to the individual. The rights established are personal rights\xe2\x80\x9d). This fundamental principle goes back, in this context, to Brown itself. See Brown v. Board of Education, 349 U. S. 294, 300 (1955) (Brown II) (\xe2\x80\x9cAt stake is the personal interest of the plaintiffs in admission to public schools \xe2\x80\xa6 on a nondiscriminatory basis\xe2\x80\x9d (emphasis added)). For the dissent, in contrast, \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98individualized scrutiny\xe2\x80\x99 is simply beside the point.\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 55.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Breyer\xe2\x80\x99s position comes down to a familiar claim: The end justifies the means. He admits that \xe2\x80\x9cthere is a cost in applying \xe2\x80\x98a state-mandated racial label,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d post, at 67, but he is confident that the cost is worth paying. Our established strict scrutiny test for racial classifications, however, insists on \xe2\x80\x9cdetailed examination, both as to ends and as to means.\xe2\x80\x9d Adarand, supra, at 236 (emphasis added). Simply because the school districts may seek a worthy goal does not mean they are free to discriminate on the basis of race to achieve it, or that their racial classifications should be subject to less exacting scrutiny.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Despite his argument that these cases should be evaluated under a \xe2\x80\x9cstandard of review that is not \xe2\x80\x98strict\xe2\x80\x99 in the traditional sense of that word,\xe2\x80\x9d post, at 36, Justice Breyer still purports to apply strict scrutiny to these cases. See post, at 37. It is evident, however, that Justice Breyer\xe2\x80\x99s brand of narrow tailoring is quite unlike anything found in our precedents. Without any detailed discussion of the operation of the plans, the students who are affected, or the districts\xe2\x80\x99 failure to consider race-neutral alternatives, the dissent concludes that the districts have shown that these racial classifications are necessary to achieve the districts\xe2\x80\x99 stated goals. This conclusion is divorced from any evaluation of the actual impact of the plans at issue in these cases\xe2\x80\x94other than to note that the plans \xe2\x80\x9coften have no effect.\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 46.[Footnote 17] Instead, the dissent suggests that some combination of the development of these plans over time, the difficulty of the endeavor, and the good faith of the districts suffices to demonstrate that these stark and controlling racial classifications are constitutional. The Constitution and our precedents require more.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In keeping with his view that strict scrutiny should not apply, Justice Breyer repeatedly urges deference to local school boards on these issues. See, e.g., post, at 21, 48\xe2\x80\x9349, 66. Such deference \xe2\x80\x9cis fundamentally at odds with our equal protection jurisprudence. We put the burden on state actors to demonstrate that their race-based policies are justified.\xe2\x80\x9d Johnson, 543 U. S., at 506, n.\xc2\xa01. See Croson, 488 U. S., at 501 (\xe2\x80\x9cThe history of racial classifications in this country suggests that blind judicial deference to legislative or executive pronouncements of necessity has no place in equal protection analysis\xe2\x80\x9d); West Virginia Bd. of Ed. v. Barnette, 319 U. S. 624, 637 (1943) (\xe2\x80\x9cThe Fourteenth Amendment \xe2\x80\xa6 protects the citizen against the State itself and all of its creatures\xe2\x80\x94Boards of Education not excepted\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Breyer\xe2\x80\x99s dissent ends on an unjustified note of alarm. It predicts that today\xe2\x80\x99s decision \xe2\x80\x9cthreaten[s]\xe2\x80\x9d the validity of \xe2\x80\x9c[h]undreds of state and federal statutes and regulations.\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 61; see also post, at 27\xe2\x80\x9328. But the examples the dissent mentions\xe2\x80\x94for example, a provision of the No Child Left Behind Act that requires States to set measurable objectives to track the achievement of students from major racial and ethnic groups, 20 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa76311(b)(2)(C)(v)\xe2\x80\x94have nothing to do with the pertinent issues in these cases.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Breyer also suggests that other means for achieving greater racial diversity in schools are necessarily unconstitutional if the racial classifications at issue in these cases cannot survive strict scrutiny. Post, at 58\xe2\x80\x9362. These other means\xe2\x80\x94e.g., where to construct new schools, how to allocate resources among schools, and which academic offerings to provide to attract students to certain schools\xe2\x80\x94implicate different considerations than the explicit racial classifications at issue in these cases, and we express no opinion on their validity\xe2\x80\x94not even in dicta. Rather, we employ the familiar and well-established analytic approach of strict scrutiny to evaluate the plans at issue today, an approach that in no way warrants the dissent\xe2\x80\x99s cataclysmic concerns. Under that approach, the school districts have not carried their burden of showing that the ends they seek justify the particular extreme means they have chosen\xe2\x80\x94classifying individual students on the basis of their race and discriminating among them on that basis.

\n

*\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83*\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83*

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0If the need for the racial classifications embraced by the school districts is unclear, even on the districts\xe2\x80\x99 own terms, the costs are undeniable. \xe2\x80\x9c[D]istinctions between citizens solely because of their ancestry are by their very nature odious to a free people whose institutions are founded upon the doctrine of equality.\xe2\x80\x9d Adarand, 515 U. S., at 214 (internal quotation marks omitted). Government action dividing us by race is inherently suspect because such classifications promote \xe2\x80\x9cnotions of racial inferiority and lead to a politics of racial hostility,\xe2\x80\x9d Croson, supra, at 493, \xe2\x80\x9creinforce the belief, held by too many for too much of our history, that individuals should be judged by the color of their skin,\xe2\x80\x9d Shaw v. Reno, 509 U. S. 630, 657 (1993), and \xe2\x80\x9cendorse race-based reasoning and the conception of a Nation divided into racial blocs, thus contributing to an escalation of racial hostility and conflict.\xe2\x80\x9d Metro Broadcasting, 497 U. S., at 603 (O\xe2\x80\x99Connor, J., dissenting). As the Court explained in Rice v. Cayetano, 528 U. S. 495, 517 (2000), \xe2\x80\x9c[o]ne of the principal reasons race is treated as a forbidden classification is that it demeans the dignity and worth of a person to be judged by ancestry instead of by his or her own merit and essential qualities.\xe2\x80\x9d

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0All this is true enough in the contexts in which these statements were made\xe2\x80\x94government contracting, voting districts, allocation of broadcast licenses, and electing state officers\xe2\x80\x94but when it comes to using race to assign children to schools, history will be heard. In Brown v. Board of Education, 347 U. S. 483 (1954) (Brown I), we held that segregation deprived black children of equal educational opportunities regardless of whether school facilities and other tangible factors were equal, because government classification and separation on grounds of race themselves denoted inferiority. Id., at 493\xe2\x80\x93494. It was not the inequality of the facilities but the fact of legally separating children on the basis of race on which the Court relied to find a constitutional violation in 1954. See id., at 494 (\xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98The impact [of segregation] is greater when it has the sanction of the law\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d). The next Term, we accordingly stated that \xe2\x80\x9cfull compliance\xe2\x80\x9d with Brown I required school districts \xe2\x80\x9cto achieve a system of determining admission to the public schools on a nonracial basis.\xe2\x80\x9d Brown II, 349 U. S., at 300\xe2\x80\x93301 (emphasis added).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The parties and their amici debate which side is more faithful to the heritage of Brown, but the position of the plaintiffs in Brown was spelled out in their brief and could not have been clearer: \xe2\x80\x9c[T]he Fourteenth Amendment prevents states from according differential treatment to American children on the basis of their color or race.\xe2\x80\x9d Brief for Appellants in Nos. 1, 2, and 4 and for Respondents in No. 10 on Reargument in Brown I, O.\xc2\xa0T. 1953, p.\xc2\xa015 (Summary of Argument). What do the racial classifications at issue here do, if not accord differential treatment on the basis of race? As counsel who appeared before this Court for the plaintiffs in Brown put it: \xe2\x80\x9cWe have one fundamental contention which we will seek to develop in the course of this argument, and that contention is that no State has any authority under the equal-protection clause of the Fourteenth Amendment to use race as a factor in affording educational opportunities among its citizens.\xe2\x80\x9d Tr. of Oral Arg. in Brown I, p. 7 (Robert L. Carter, Dec. 9, 1952). There is no ambiguity in that statement. And it was that position that prevailed in this Court, which emphasized in its remedial opinion that what was \xe2\x80\x9c[a]t stake is the personal interest of the plaintiffs in admission to public schools as soon as practicable on a nondiscriminatory basis,\xe2\x80\x9d and what was required was \xe2\x80\x9cdetermining admission to the public schools on a nonracial basis.\xe2\x80\x9d Brown II, supra, at 300\xe2\x80\x93301 (emphasis added). What do the racial classifications do in these cases, if not determine admission to a public school on a racial basis?\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Before Brown, schoolchildren were told where they could and could not go to school based on the color of their skin. The school districts in these cases have not carried the heavy burden of demonstrating that we should allow this once again\xe2\x80\x94even for very different reasons. For schools that never segregated on the basis of race, such as Seattle, or that have removed the vestiges of past segregation, such as Jefferson County, the way \xe2\x80\x9cto achieve a system of determining admission to the public schools on a nonracial basis,\xe2\x80\x9d Brown II, 349 U. S., at 300\xe2\x80\x93301, is to stop assigning students on a racial basis. The way to stop discrimination on the basis of race is to stop discriminating on the basis of race.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The judgments of the Courts of Appeals for the Sixth and Ninth Circuits are reversed, and the cases are\nremanded for further proceedings.

\n

It is so ordered.

\nFootnote 10

\xc2\xa0The districts point to dicta in a prior opinion in which the Court suggested that, while not constitutionally mandated, it would be constitutionally permissible for a school district to seek racially balanced schools as a matter of \xe2\x80\x9ceducational policy.\xe2\x80\x9d See Swann v. Charlotte-Mecklenburg Bd. of Ed., 402 U. S. 1, 16 (1971). The districts also quote with approval an in-chambers opinion in which then-Justice Rehnquist made a suggestion to the same effect. See Bustop, Inc. v. Los Angeles Bd. of Ed., 439 U. S. 1380, 1383 (1978). The citations do not carry the significance the districts would ascribe to them. Swann, evaluating a school district engaged in court-ordered desegregation, had no occasion to consider whether a district\xe2\x80\x99s voluntary adoption of race-based assignments in the absence of a finding of prior de jure segregation was constitutionally permissible, an issue that was again expressly reserved in Washington v. Seattle School Dist. No. 1, 458 U. S. 457, 472, n. 15 (1982). Bustop, addressing in the context of an emergency injunction application a busing plan imposed by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County, is similarly unavailing. Then-Justice Rehnquist, in denying emergency relief, stressed that \xe2\x80\x9cequitable consideration[s]\xe2\x80\x9d counseled against preliminary relief. 439 U. S., at 1383. The propriety of preliminary relief and resolution of the merits are of course \xe2\x80\x9csignificantly different\xe2\x80\x9d issues. University of Texas v. Camenisch, 451 U. S. 390, 393 (1981).

\nFootnote 11

\nFootnote 12

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Jefferson County also argues that it would be incongruous to hold that what was constitutionally required of it one day\xe2\x80\x94race-based assignments pursuant to the desegregation decree\xe2\x80\x94can be constitutionally prohibited the next. But what was constitutionally required of the district prior to 2000 was the elimination of the vestiges of prior segregation\xe2\x80\x94not racial proportionality in its own right. See Freeman v. Pitts, 503 U. S. 467, 494\xe2\x80\x93496 (1992). Once those vestiges were eliminated, Jefferson County was on the same footing as any other school district, and its use of race must be justified on other grounds.

\nFootnote 13
