As to \xe2\x80\x9callocating resources for special programs,\xe2\x80\x9d Seattle and Louisville have both experimented with this; indeed, these programs are often referred to as \xe2\x80\x9cmagnet schools,\xe2\x80\x9d but the limited desegregation effect of these efforts extends at most to those few schools to which additional resources are granted. In addition, there is no evidence from the experience of these school districts that it will make any meaningful impact. See Brief for Respondents in No. 05\xe2\x80\x93908, p. 42. As to \xe2\x80\x9crecruiting faculty\xe2\x80\x9d on the basis of race, both cities have tried, but only as one part of a broader program. As to \xe2\x80\x9ctracking enrollments, performance and other statistics by race,\xe2\x80\x9d tracking reveals the problem; it does not cure it.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Kennedy sets forth two additional concerns related to \xe2\x80\x9cnarrow tailoring.\xe2\x80\x9d In respect to Louisville, he says first that officials stated (1) that kindergarten assignments are not subject to the race-conscious guidelines, and (2) that the child at issue here was denied permission to attend the kindergarten he wanted because of those guidelines. Both, he explains, cannot be true. He adds that this confusion illustrates that Louisville\xe2\x80\x99s assignment plan (or its explanation of it to this Court) is insufficiently precise in respect to \xe2\x80\x9cwho makes the decisions,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9coversight,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cthe precise circumstances in which an assignment decision\xe2\x80\x9d will be made; and \xe2\x80\x9cwhich of two similarly situated children will be subjected to a given race-based decision.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 4.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The record suggests, however, that the child in question was not assigned to the school he preferred because he missed the kindergarten application deadline. See App. in 05\xe2\x80\x93915, p. 20. After he had enrolled and after the academic year had begun, he then applied to transfer to his preferred school after the kindergarten assignment deadline had passed, id., at 21, possibly causing school officials to treat his late request as an application to transfer to the first grade, in respect to which the guidelines apply. I am not certain just how the remainder of Justice Kennedy\xe2\x80\x99s concerns affect the lawfulness of the Louisville program, for they seem to be failures of explanation, not of administration. But Louisville should be able to answer the relevant questions on remand.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Kennedy\xe2\x80\x99s second concern is directly related to the merits of Seattle\xe2\x80\x99s plan: Why does Seattle\xe2\x80\x99s plan group Asian-Americans, Hispanic-Americans, Native-Americans, and African-Americans together, treating all as similar minorities? Ante, at 6\xe2\x80\x937. The majority suggests that Seattle\xe2\x80\x99s classification system could permit a school to be labeled \xe2\x80\x9cdiverse\xe2\x80\x9d with a 50% Asian-American and 50% white student body, and no African-American students, Hispanic students, or students of other ethnicity. Ante, at 6; ante, at 15\xe2\x80\x9316 (opinion of the Court).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The 50/50 hypothetical has no support in the record here; it is conjured from the imagination. In fact, Seattle apparently began to treat these different minority groups alike in response to the federal Emergency School Aid Act\xe2\x80\x99s requirement that it do so. Siqueland 116\xe2\x80\x93117. See also Hanawalt 31; Pub. L. 95\xe2\x80\x93561, Tit. VI (1978) (prescribing percentage enrollment requirements for \xe2\x80\x9cminority\xe2\x80\x9d students); Siqueland 55 (discussing HEW definition of \xe2\x80\x9cminority\xe2\x80\x9d). Moreover, maintaining this federally mandated system of classification makes sense insofar as Seattle\xe2\x80\x99s experience indicates that the relevant circumstances in respect to each of these different minority groups are roughly similar, e.g., in terms of residential patterns, and call for roughly similar responses. This is confirmed by the fact that Seattle has been able to achieve a desirable degree of diversity without the greater emphasis on race that drawing fine lines among minority groups would require. Does the plurality\xe2\x80\x99s view of the Equal Protection Clause mean that courts must give no weight to such a board determination? Does it insist upon especially strong evidence supporting inclusion of multiple minority groups in an otherwise lawful government minority-assistance program? If so, its interpretation threatens to produce divisiveness among minority groups that is incompatible with the basic objectives of the Fourteenth Amendment. Regardless, the plurality cannot object that the constitutional defect is the individualized use of race and simultaneously object that not enough account of individuals\xe2\x80\x99 race has been taken.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Finally, I recognize that the Court seeks to distinguish Grutter from these cases by claiming that Grutter arose in \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98the context of higher education.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 16. But that is not a meaningful legal distinction. I have explained why I do not believe the Constitution could possibly find \xe2\x80\x9ccompelling\xe2\x80\x9d the provision of a racially diverse education for a 23-year-old law student but not for a 13-year-old high school pupil. See supra, at 46\xe2\x80\x9348. And I have explained how the plans before us are more narrowly tailored than those in Grutter. See supra, at 45. I add that one cannot find a relevant distinction in the fact that these school districts did not examine the merits of applications \xe2\x80\x9cindividual[ly].\xe2\x80\x9d See ante, at 13\xe2\x80\x9315. The context here does not involve admission by merit; a child\xe2\x80\x99s academic, artistic, and athletic \xe2\x80\x9cmerits\xe2\x80\x9d are not at all relevant to the child\xe2\x80\x99s placement. These are not affirmative action plans, and hence \xe2\x80\x9cindividualized scrutiny\xe2\x80\x9d is simply beside the point.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The upshot is that these plans\xe2\x80\x99 specific features\xe2\x80\x94(1) their limited and historically-diminishing use of race, (2) their strong reliance upon other non-race-conscious elements, (3) their history and the manner in which the districts developed and modified their approach, (4) the comparison with prior plans, and (5) the lack of reasonably evident alternatives\xe2\x80\x94together show that the districts\xe2\x80\x99 plans are \xe2\x80\x9cnarrowly tailored\xe2\x80\x9d to achieve their \xe2\x80\x9ccompelling\xe2\x80\x9d goals. In sum, the districts\xe2\x80\x99 race-conscious plans satisfy \xe2\x80\x9cstrict scrutiny\xe2\x80\x9d and are therefore lawful.

\n

IV

\n

Direct Precedent

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Two additional precedents more directly related to the plans here at issue reinforce my conclusion. The first consists of the District Court determination in the Louisville case when it dissolved its desegregation order that there was \xe2\x80\x9coverwhelming evidence of the Board\xe2\x80\x99s good faith compliance with the desegregation Decree and its underlying purposes,\xe2\x80\x9d indeed that the Board had \xe2\x80\x9ctreated the ideal of an integrated system as much more than a legal obligation\xe2\x80\x94they consider it a positive, desirable policy and an essential element of any well-rounded public school education.\xe2\x80\x9d Hampton II, 102 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 370. When the court made this determination in 2000, it did so in the context of the Louisville desegregation plan that the board had adopted in 1996. That plan, which took effect before 1996, is the very plan that in all relevant respects is in effect now and is the subject of the present challenge.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0No one claims that (the relevant portion of) Louisville\xe2\x80\x99s plan was unlawful in 1996 when Louisville adopted it. To the contrary, there is every reason to believe that it represented part of an effort to implement the 1978 desegregation order. But if the plan was lawful when it was first adopted and if it was lawful the day before the District Court dissolved its order, how can the plurality now suggest that it became unlawful the following day? Is it conceivable that the Constitution, implemented through a court desegregation order, could permit (perhaps require) the district to make use of a race-conscious plan the day before the order was dissolved and then forbid the district to use the identical plan the day after? See id., at 380 (\xe2\x80\x9cThe very analysis for dissolving desegregation decrees supports continued maintenance of a desegregated system as a compelling state interest\xe2\x80\x9d). The Equal Protection Clause is not incoherent. And federal courts would rightly hesitate to find unitary status if the consequences of the ruling were so dramatically disruptive.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Second, Seattle School Dist. No. 1, 458 U. S. 457, is directly on point. That case involves the original Seattle Plan, a more heavily race-conscious predecessor of the very plan now before us. In Seattle School Dist. No. 1, this Court struck down a state referendum that effectively barred implementation of Seattle\xe2\x80\x99s desegregation plan and \xe2\x80\x9cburden[ed] all future attempts to integrate Washington schools in districts throughout the State.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 462\xe2\x80\x93463, 483. Because the referendum would have prohibited the adoption of a school-integration plan that involved mandatory busing, and because it would have imposed a special burden on school integration plans (plans that sought to integrate previously segregated schools), the Court found it unconstitutional. Id., at 483\xe2\x80\x93487.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In reaching this conclusion, the Court did not directly address the constitutional merits of the underlying Seattle plan. But it explicitly cited Swann\xe2\x80\x99s statement that the Constitution permitted a local district to adopt such a plan. 458 U. S., at 472, n.\xc2\xa015. It also cited to Justice Powell\xe2\x80\x99s opinion in Bakke, approving of the limited use of race-conscious criteria in a university-admissions \xe2\x80\x9caffirmative action\xe2\x80\x9d case. 458 U. S., at 472, n. 15. In addition, the Court stated that \xe2\x80\x9c[a]ttending an ethnically diverse school,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 473, could help prepare \xe2\x80\x9cminority children for citizenship in our pluralistic society,\xe2\x80\x9d hopefully \xe2\x80\x9cteaching members of the racial majority to live in harmony and mutual respect with children of minority heritage.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. (internal quotation marks and citation omitted).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0It is difficult to believe that the Court that held unconstitutional a referendum that would have interfered with the implementation of this plan thought that the integration plan it sought to preserve was itself an unconstitutional plan. And if Seattle School Dist. No. 1 is premised upon the constitutionality of the original Seattle Plan, it is equally premised upon the constitutionality of the present plan, for the present plan is the Seattle Plan, modified only insofar as it places even less emphasis on race-conscious elements than its predecessors.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0It is even more difficult to accept the plurality\xe2\x80\x99s contrary view, namely that the underlying plan was unconstitutional. If that is so, then all of Seattle\xe2\x80\x99s earlier (even more race-conscious) plans must also have been unconstitutional. That necessary implication of the plurality\xe2\x80\x99s position strikes the 13th chime of the clock. How could the plurality adopt a constitutional standard that would hold unconstitutional large numbers of race-conscious integration plans adopted by numerous school boards over the past 50 years while remaining true to this Court\xe2\x80\x99s desegregation precedent?

\n

V

\n

Consequences

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Founders meant the Constitution as a practical document that would transmit its basic values to future generations through principles that remained workable over time. Hence it is important to consider the potential consequences of the plurality\xe2\x80\x99s approach, as measured against the Constitution\xe2\x80\x99s objectives. \xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0To do so provides further reason to believe that the plurality\xe2\x80\x99s approach is legally unsound.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0For one thing, consider the effect of the plurality\xe2\x80\x99s views on the parties before us and on similar school districts throughout the Nation. Will Louisville and all similar school districts have to return to systems like Louisville\xe2\x80\x99s initial 1956 plan, which did not consider race at all? See supra, at 12. That initial 1956 plan proved ineffective. Sixteen years into the plan, 14 of 19 middle and high schools remained almost totally white or almost totally black. Ibid.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The districts\xe2\x80\x99 past and current plans are not unique. They resemble other plans, promulgated by hundreds of local school boards, which have attempted a variety of desegregation methods that have evolved over time in light of experience. A 1987 Civil Rights Commission Study of 125 school districts in the Nation demonstrated the breadth and variety of desegregation plans:

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cThe [study] documents almost 300 desegregation plans that were implemented between 1961 and 1985. The degree of heterogeneity within these districts is immediately apparent. They are located in every region of the country and range in size from Las Cruces, New Mexico, with barely over 15,000 students attending 23 schools in 1968, to New York City, with more than one million students in 853 schools. The sample includes districts in urban areas of all sizes, suburbs (e.g., Arlington County, Virginia) and rural areas (e.g., Jefferson Parish, Louisiana, and Raleigh County, West Virginia). It contains 34 countywide districts with central cities (the 11 Florida districts fit this description, plus Clark County, Nevada and others) and a small number of consolidated districts (New Castle County, Delaware and Jefferson County, Kentucky).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cThe districts also vary in their racial compositions and levels of segregation. Initial plans were implemented in Mobile, Alabama and Mecklenburg County, North Carolina, and in a number of other southern districts in the face of total racial segregation. At the other extreme, Santa Clara, California had a relatively even racial distribution prior to its 1979 desegregation plan. When the 1965 plan was designed for Harford County, Maryland, the district was 92 percent white. Compton, California, on the other hand, became over 99 percent black in the 1980s, while Buffalo, New York had a virtual 50\xe2\x80\x9350 split between white and minority students prior to its 1977 plan.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not surprising to find a large number of different desegregation strategies in a sample with this much variation.\xe2\x80\x9d Welch 23 (footnotes omitted).

\n

A majority of these desegregation techniques explicitly considered a student\xe2\x80\x99s race. See id., at 24\xe2\x80\x9328. Transfer plans, for example, allowed students to shift from a school in which they were in the racial majority to a school in which they would be in a racial minority. Some districts, such as Richmond, California, and Buffalo, New York, permitted only \xe2\x80\x9cone-way\xe2\x80\x9d transfers, in which only black students attending predominantly black schools were permitted to transfer to designated receiver schools. Id., at 25. Fifty-three of the 125 studied districts used transfers as a component of their plans. Id., at 83\xe2\x80\x9391.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0At the state level, 46 States and Puerto Rico have adopted policies that encourage or require local school districts to enact interdistrict or intradistrict open choice plans. Eight of those States condition approval of transfers to another school or district on whether the transfer will produce increased racial integration. Eleven other States require local boards to deny transfers that are\nnot in compliance with the local school board\xe2\x80\x99s desegre-\ngation plans. See Education Commission of the States, Open Enrollment: 50-State Report (2007), online at http://mb2.ecs.org/reports/Report.aspx?id=268.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Arkansas, for example, provides by statute that \xe2\x80\x9c[n]o student may transfer to a nonresident district where the percentage of enrollment for the student\xe2\x80\x99s race exceeds that percentage in the student\xe2\x80\x99s resident district.\xe2\x80\x9d Ark. Code Ann. \xc2\xa76\xe2\x80\x9318\xe2\x80\x93206(f)(1), as amended 2007 Ark. Gen. Acts 552 (2007). An Ohio statute provides, in respect to student choice, that each school district must establish \xe2\x80\x9c[p]rocedures to ensure that an appropriate racial balance is maintained in the district schools.\xe2\x80\x9d Ohio Rev. Code Ann. \xc2\xa73313.98(B)(2)(b)(iii) (Lexis Supp. 2006). Ohio adds that a \xe2\x80\x9cdistrict may object to the enrollment of a native student in an adjacent or other district in order to maintain an appropriate racial balance.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa73313.98 (F)(1)(a).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0A Connecticut statute states that its student choice program will seek to \xe2\x80\x9cpreserve racial and ethnic balance.\xe2\x80\x9d Conn. Gen. Stat. \xc2\xa710\xe2\x80\x93266aa(b)(2) (2007). Connecticut law requires each school district to submit racial group population figures to the State Board of Education. \xc2\xa710\xe2\x80\x93226a. Another Connecticut regulation provides that \xe2\x80\x9c[a]ny school in which the Proportion for the School falls outside of a range from 25 percentage points less to 25 percentage points more than the Comparable Proportion for the School District, shall be determined to be racially imbalanced.\xe2\x80\x9d Conn. Agencies Regs. \xc2\xa710\xe2\x80\x93226e\xe2\x80\x933(b) (1999). A \xe2\x80\x9cracial imbalance\xe2\x80\x9d determination requires the district to submit a plan to correct the racial imbalance, which plan may include \xe2\x80\x9cmandatory pupil reassignment.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa710\xe2\x80\x93226e\xe2\x80\x935(a) and (c)(4).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Interpreting that State\xe2\x80\x99s Constitution, the Connecticut Supreme Court has held legally inadequate the reliance by a local school district solely upon some of the techniques Justice Kennedy today recommends (e.g., reallocating resources, etc.). See Sheff v. O\xe2\x80\x99Neill, 238 Conn. 1, 678 A.\xc2\xa02d 1267 (1996). The State Supreme Court wrote: \xe2\x80\x9cDespite the initiatives undertaken by the defendants to alleviate the severe racial and ethnic disparities among school districts, and despite the fact that the defendants did not intend to create or maintain these disparities, the disparities that continue to burden the education of the plaintiffs infringe upon their fundamental state constitutional right to a substantially equal educational opportunity.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 42, 678 A.\xc2\xa02d, at 1289.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0At a minimum, the plurality\xe2\x80\x99s views would threaten a surge of race-based litigation. Hundreds of state and federal statutes and regulations use racial classifications for educational or other purposes. See supra, at 27. In many such instances, the contentious force of legal challenges to these classifications, meritorious or not, would displace earlier calm.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The wide variety of different integration plans that school districts use throughout the Nation suggests that the problem of racial segregation in schools, including de facto segregation, is difficult to solve. The fact that many such plans have used explicitly racial criteria suggests that such criteria have an important, sometimes necessary, role to play. The fact that the controlling opinion would make a school district\xe2\x80\x99s use of such criteria often unlawful (and the plurality\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9ccolorblind\xe2\x80\x9d view would make such use always unlawful) suggests that today\xe2\x80\x99s opinion will require setting aside the laws of several States and many local communities.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0As I have pointed out, supra, at 4, de facto resegregation is on the rise. See Appendix A, infra. It is reasonable to conclude that such resegregation can create serious educational, social, and civic problems. See supra, at 37\xe2\x80\x9345. Given the conditions in which school boards work to set policy, see supra, at 20\xe2\x80\x9321, they may need all of the means presently at their disposal to combat those problems. Yet the plurality would deprive them of at least one tool that some districts now consider vital\xe2\x80\x94the limited use of broad race-conscious student population ranges.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0I use the words \xe2\x80\x9cmay need\xe2\x80\x9d here deliberately. The plurality, or at least those who follow Justice Thomas\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98color-blind\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d approach, see ante, at 26\xe2\x80\x9327 (Thomas, J., concurring); Grutter, 539 U. S., at 353\xe2\x80\x93354 (Thomas, J., concurring in part and dissenting in part), may feel confident that, to end invidious discrimination, one must end all governmental use of race-conscious criteria including those with inclusive objectives. See ante, at 40\xe2\x80\x9341 (plurality opinion); see also ante, at 26 (Thomas, J., concurring). By way of contrast, I do not claim to know how best to stop harmful discrimination; how best to create a society that includes all Americans; how best to overcome our serious problems of increasing de facto segregation, troubled inner city schooling, and poverty correlated with race. But, as a judge, I do know that the Constitution does not authorize judges to dictate solutions to these problems. Rather, the Constitution creates a democratic political system through which the people themselves must together find answers. And it is for them to debate how best to educate the Nation\xe2\x80\x99s children and how best to administer America\xe2\x80\x99s schools to achieve that aim. The Court should leave them to their work. And it is for them to decide, to quote the plurality\xe2\x80\x99s slogan, whether the best \xe2\x80\x9cway to stop discrimination on the basis of race is to stop discriminating on the basis of race.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 40\xe2\x80\x9341. See also Parents Involved VII, 426 F.\xc2\xa03d, at 1222 (Bea, J., dissenting) (\xe2\x80\x9cThe way to end racial discrimination is to stop discriminating by race\xe2\x80\x9d). That is why the Equal Protection Clause outlaws invidious discrimination, but does not similarly forbid all use of race-conscious criteria.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Until today, this Court understood the Constitution as affording the people, acting through their elected representatives, freedom to select the use of \xe2\x80\x9crace-conscious\xe2\x80\x9d criteria from among their available options. See Adarand Constructors, Inc., 515 U. S., at 237 (\xe2\x80\x9c[S]trict scrutiny\xe2\x80\x9d in this context is \xe2\x80\x9c[not] \xe2\x80\x98strict in theory, but fatal in fact\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d (quoting Fullilove, 448 U. S., at 519 (Marshall, J., concurring in judgment))). Today, however, the Court restricts (and some Members would eliminate) that leeway. I fear the consequences of doing so for the law, for the schools, for the democratic process, and for America\xe2\x80\x99s efforts to create, out of its diversity, one Nation.

\n

VI

\n

Conclusions

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0To show that the school assignment plans here meet the requirements of the Constitution, I have written at exceptional length. But that length is necessary. I cannot refer to the history of the plans in these cases to justify the use of race-conscious criteria without describing that history in full. I cannot rely upon Swann\xe2\x80\x99s statement that the use of race-conscious limits is permissible without showing, rather than simply asserting, that the statement represents a constitutional principle firmly rooted in federal and state law. Nor can I explain my disagreement with the Court\xe2\x80\x99s holding and the plurality\xe2\x80\x99s opinion, without offering a detailed account of the arguments they propound and the consequences they risk.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Thus, the opinion\xe2\x80\x99s reasoning is long. But its conclusion is short: The plans before us satisfy the requirements of the Equal Protection Clause. And it is the plurality\xe2\x80\x99s opinion, not this dissent that \xe2\x80\x9cfails to ground the result it would reach in law.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 28.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Four basic considerations have led me to this view. First, the histories of Louisville and Seattle reveal complex circumstances and a long tradition of conscientious efforts by local school boards to resist racial segregation in public schools. Segregation at the time of Brown gave way to expansive remedies that included busing, which in turn gave rise to fears of white flight and resegregation. For decades now, these school boards have considered and adopted and revised assignment plans that sought to rely less upon race, to emphasize greater student choice, and to improve the conditions of all schools for all students, no matter the color of their skin, no matter where they happen to reside. The plans under review\xe2\x80\x94which are less burdensome, more egalitarian, and more effective than prior plans\xe2\x80\x94continue in that tradition. And their history reveals school district goals whose remedial, educational, and democratic elements are inextricably intertwined each with the others. See Part I, supra, at 2\xe2\x80\x9321.

\n

\nSecond, since this Court\xe2\x80\x99s decision in Brown, the law has consistently and unequivocally approved of both voluntary and compulsory race-conscious measures to combat segregated schools. The Equal Protection Clause, ratified following the Civil War, has always distinguished in practice between state action that excludes and thereby subordinates racial minorities and state action that seeks to bring together people of all races. From Swann to Grutter, this Court\xe2\x80\x99s decisions have emphasized this distinction, recognizing that the fate of race relations in this country depends upon unity among our children, \xe2\x80\x9cfor unless our children begin to learn together, there is little hope that our people will ever learn to live together.\xe2\x80\x9d Milliken, 418 U. S., at 783 (Marshall, J., dissenting). See also C. Sumner, Equality Before the Law: Unconstitutionality of Separate Colored Schools in Massachusetts, in 2 The Works of Charles Sumner 327, 371 (1849) (\xe2\x80\x9cThe law contemplates not only that all be taught, but that all shall be taught together\xe2\x80\x9d). See Part II, supra, at 21\xe2\x80\x9337.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Third, the plans before us, subjected to rigorous judicial review, are supported by compelling state interests and are narrowly tailored to accomplish those goals. Just as diversity in higher education was deemed compelling in Grutter, diversity in public primary and secondary schools\xe2\x80\x94where there is even more to gain\xe2\x80\x94must be, a fortiori, a compelling state interest. Even apart from Grutter, five Members of this Court agree that \xe2\x80\x9cavoiding racial isolation\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cachiev[ing] a diverse student population\xe2\x80\x9d remain today compelling interests. Ante, at 17\xe2\x80\x9318 (opinion of Kennedy, J.). These interests combine remedial, educational, and democratic objectives. For the reasons discussed above, however, I disagree with Justice Kennedy that Seattle and Louisville have not done enough to demonstrate that their present plans are necessary to continue upon the path set by Brown. These plans are more \xe2\x80\x9cnarrowly tailored\xe2\x80\x9d than the race-conscious law school admissions criteria at issue in Grutter. Hence, their lawfulness follows a fortiori from this Court\xe2\x80\x99s prior decisions. See Parts III\xe2\x80\x93IV, supra, at 37\xe2\x80\x9357.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Fourth, the plurality\xe2\x80\x99s approach risks serious harm to the law and for the Nation. Its view of the law rests either upon a denial of the distinction between exclusionary and inclusive use of race-conscious criteria in the context of the Equal Protection Clause, or upon such a rigid application of its \xe2\x80\x9ctest\xe2\x80\x9d that the distinction loses practical significance. Consequently, the Court\xe2\x80\x99s decision today slows down and sets back the work of local school boards to bring about racially diverse schools. See Part V, supra, at 57\xe2\x80\x9363.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Indeed, the consequences of the approach the Court takes today are serious. Yesterday, the plans under review were lawful. Today, they are not. Yesterday, the citizens of this Nation could look for guidance to this Court\xe2\x80\x99s unanimous pronouncements concerning desegregation. Today, they cannot. Yesterday, school boards had available to them a full range of means to combat segregated schools. Today, they do not.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court\xe2\x80\x99s decision undermines other basic institutional principles as well. What has happened to stare decisis? The history of the plans before us, their educational importance, their highly limited use of race\xe2\x80\x94all these and more\xe2\x80\x94make clear that the compelling interest here is stronger than in Grutter. The plans here are more narrowly tailored than the law school admissions program there at issue. Hence, applying Grutter\xe2\x80\x99s strict test, their lawfulness follows a fortiori. To hold to the contrary is to transform that test from \xe2\x80\x9cstrict\xe2\x80\x9d to \xe2\x80\x9cfatal in fact\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94the very opposite of what Grutter said. And what has happened to Swann? To McDaniel? To Crawford? To Harris? To School Committee of Boston? To Seattle School Dist. No. 1? After decades of vibrant life, they would all, under the plurality\xe2\x80\x99s logic, be written out of the law.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0And what of respect for democratic local decisionmaking by States and school boards? For several decades this Court has rested its public school decisions upon Swann\xe2\x80\x99s basic view that the Constitution grants local school districts a significant degree of leeway where the inclusive use of race-conscious criteria is at issue. Now localities will have to cope with the difficult problems they face (including resegregation) deprived of one means they may find necessary.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0And what of law\xe2\x80\x99s concern to diminish and peacefully settle conflict among the Nation\xe2\x80\x99s people? Instead of accommodating different good-faith visions of our country and our Constitution, today\xe2\x80\x99s holding upsets settled expectations, creates legal uncertainty, and threatens to produce considerable further litigation, aggravating race-related conflict.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0And what of the long history and moral vision that the Fourteenth Amendment itself embodies? The plurality cites in support those who argued in Brown against segregation, and Justice Thomas likens the approach that I have taken to that of segregation\xe2\x80\x99s defenders. See ante, at 39\xe2\x80\x9341 (plurality opinion) (comparing Jim Crow segregation to Seattle and Louisville\xe2\x80\x99s integration polices); ante, at 28\xe2\x80\x9332 (Thomas, J., concurring). But segregation policies did not simply tell schoolchildren \xe2\x80\x9cwhere they could and could not go to school based on the color of their skin,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 40 (plurality opinion); they perpetuated a caste system rooted in the institutions of slavery and 80 years of legalized subordination. The lesson of history, see ante, at 39 (plurality opinion), is not that efforts to continue racial segregation are constitutionally indistinguishable from efforts to achieve racial integration. Indeed, it is a cruel distortion of history to compare Topeka, Kansas, in the 1950\xe2\x80\x99s to Louisville and Seattle in the modern day\xe2\x80\x94to equate the plight of Linda Brown (who was ordered to attend a Jim Crow school) to the circumstances of Joshua McDonald (whose request to transfer to a school closer to home was initially declined). This is not to deny that there is a cost in applying \xe2\x80\x9ca state-mandated racial label.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 17 (Kennedy, J., concurring in part and concurring in judgment). But that cost does not approach, in degree or in kind, the terrible harms of slavery, the resulting caste system, and 80 years of legal racial segregation.

\n

*\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83*\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83*

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Finally, what of the hope and promise of Brown? For much of this Nation\xe2\x80\x99s history, the races remained divided. It was not long ago that people of different races drank from separate fountains, rode on separate buses, and studied in separate schools. In this Court\xe2\x80\x99s finest hour, Brown v. Board of Education challenged this history and helped to change it. For Brown held out a promise. It was a promise embodied in three Amendments designed to make citizens of slaves. It was the promise of true racial equality\xe2\x80\x94not as a matter of fine words on paper, but as a matter of everyday life in the Nation\xe2\x80\x99s cities and schools. It was about the nature of a democracy that must work for all Americans. It sought one law, one Nation, one people, not simply as a matter of legal principle but in terms of how we actually live.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Not everyone welcomed this Court\xe2\x80\x99s decision in Brown. Three years after that decision was handed down, the Governor of Arkansas ordered state militia to block the doors of a white schoolhouse so that black children could not enter. The President of the United States dispatched the 101st Airborne Division to Little Rock, Arkansas, and federal troops were needed to enforce a desegregation decree. See Cooper v. Aaron, 358 U. S. 1 (1958). Today, almost 50 years later, attitudes toward race in this Nation have changed dramatically. Many parents, white and black alike, want their children to attend schools with children of different races. Indeed, the very school districts that once spurned integration now strive for it. The long history of their efforts reveals the complexities and difficulties they have faced. And in light of those challenges, they have asked us not to take from their hands the instruments they have used to rid their schools of racial segregation, instruments that they believe are needed to overcome the problems of cities divided by race and poverty. The plurality would decline their modest request.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The plurality is wrong to do so. The last half-century has witnessed great strides toward racial equality, but we have not yet realized the promise of Brown. To invalidate the plans under review is to threaten the promise of Brown. The plurality\xe2\x80\x99s position, I fear, would break that promise. This is a decision that the Court and the Nation will come to regret.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0I must dissent.

\n

\nSTEVENS, J., DISSENTING
\nPARENTS INVOLVED IN COMMUNITY SCHOOLS V.SEATTLE SCHOOL DIST. NO. 1
\n551 U. S. ____ (2007)
\nSUPREME COURT OF THE UNITED STATES
\nNOS. 05-908 AND 05-915
\n
\n

\nPARENTS INVOLVED IN COMMUNITY\nSCHOOLS, PETITIONER

\n

\n05\xe2\x80\x93908\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0v.

\n

\nSEATTLE SCHOOL DISTRICT NO. 1 et\xc2\xa0al.

\n

on writ of certiorari to the united states court of\nappeals for the ninth circuit

\n

\nCRYSTAL D. MEREDITH, custodial parent and next\n friend of JOSHUA RYAN McDONALD, PETITIONER

\n

\n05\xe2\x80\x93915\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0v.

\n

\nJEFFERSON COUNTY BOARD OF EDUCATION et\xc2\xa0al.

\n

on writ of certiorari to the united states court of\nappeals for the sixth circuit

\n

[June 28, 2007]

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Stevens, dissenting.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0While I join Justice Breyer\xe2\x80\x99s eloquent and unanswerable dissent in its entirety, it is appropriate to add these words.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0There is a cruel irony in The Chief Justice\xe2\x80\x99s reliance on our decision in Brown v. Board of Education, 349 U. S. 294 (1955). The first sentence in the concluding paragraph of his opinion states: \xe2\x80\x9cBefore Brown, schoolchildren were told where they could and could not go to school based on the color of their skin.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 40. This sentence reminds me of Anatole France\xe2\x80\x99s observation: \xe2\x80\x9c[T]he majestic equality of the la[w], forbid[s] rich and poor alike to sleep under bridges, to beg in the streets, and to steal their bread.\xe2\x80\x9d[Footnote 1] The Chief Justice fails to note that it was only black schoolchildren who were so ordered; indeed, the history books do not tell stories of white children struggling to attend black schools.[Footnote 2] In this and other ways, The Chief Justice rewrites the history of one of this Court\xe2\x80\x99s most important decisions. Compare ante, at 39 (\xe2\x80\x9chistory will be heard\xe2\x80\x9d), with Brewer v. Quarterman, 550 U. S. ___, ___ (2007) (slip op., at 11) (Roberts, C.\xc2\xa0J., dissenting) (\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a familiar adage that history is written by the victors\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Chief Justice rejects the conclusion that the racial classifications at issue here should be viewed differently than others, because they do not impose burdens on one race alone and do not stigmatize or exclude.[Footnote 3] The only justification for refusing to acknowledge the obvious importance of that difference is the citation of a few recent opinions\xe2\x80\x94none of which even approached unanimity\xe2\x80\x94grandly proclaiming that all racial classifications must be analyzed under \xe2\x80\x9cstrict scrutiny.\xe2\x80\x9d See, e.g., Adarand Constructors, Inc. v. Pe\xc5\x84a, 515 U. S. 200, 227 (1995). Even today, two of our wisest federal judges have rejected such a wooden reading of the Equal Protection Clause in the context of school integration. See 426 F. 3d 1162, 1193\xe2\x80\x931196 (CA9 2005) (Kozinski, J., concurring); Comfort v. Lynn School Comm., 418 F.\xc2\xa03d 1, 27\xe2\x80\x9329 (CA1 2005) (Boudin, C.\xc2\xa0J., concurring). The Court\xe2\x80\x99s misuse of the three-tiered approach to Equal Protection analysis merely reconfirms my own view that there is only one such Clause in the Constitution. See Craig v. Boren, 429 U. S. 190, 211 (1976) (concurring opinion).[Footnote 4]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0If we look at cases decided during the interim between Brown and Adarand, we can see how a rigid adherence to tiers of scrutiny obscures Brown\xe2\x80\x99s clear message. Perhaps the best example is provided by our approval of the decision of the Supreme Judicial Court of Massachusetts in 1967 upholding a state statute mandating racial integration in that State\xe2\x80\x99s school system. See School Comm. of Boston v. Board of Education, 352 Mass. 693, 227 N.\xc2\xa0E.\xc2\xa02d 729.[Footnote 5] Rejecting arguments comparable to those that the plurality accepts today,[Footnote 6] that court noted: \xe2\x80\x9cIt would be the height of irony if the racial imbalance act, enacted as it was with the laudable purpose of achieving equal educational opportunities, should, by prescribing school pupil allocations based on race, founder on unsuspected shoals in the Fourteenth Amendment.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 698, 227 N.\xc2\xa0E. 2d, at 733 (footnote omitted).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Invoking our mandatory appellate jurisdiction,[Footnote 7] the Boston plaintiffs prosecuted an appeal in this Court. Our ruling on the merits simply stated that the appeal was \xe2\x80\x9cdismissed for want of a substantial federal question.\xe2\x80\x9d School Comm. of Boston v. Board of Education, 389 U. S. 572 (1968) (per curiam). That decision not only expressed our appraisal of the merits of the appeal, but it constitutes a precedent that the Court overrules today. The subsequent statements by the unanimous Court in Swann v. Charlotte-Mecklenburg Bd. of Ed., 402 U. S. 1, 16 (1971), by then-Justice Rehnquist in chambers in Bustop, Inc. v. Los Angeles Bd. of Ed., 439 U. S. 1380, 1383 (1978), and by the host of state court decisions cited by Justice Breyer, see post, 25\xe2\x80\x9327,[Footnote 8] were fully consistent with that disposition. Unlike today\xe2\x80\x99s decision, they were also entirely loyal to Brown.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court has changed significantly since it decided School Comm. of Boston in 1968. It was then more faithful to Brown and more respectful of our precedent than it is today. It is my firm conviction that no Member of the Court that I joined in 1975 would have agreed with today\xe2\x80\x99s decision.

\nFootnote 1

\xc2\xa0Le Lys Rouge (The Red Lily) 95 (W. Stephens transl. 6th ed. 1922).

\nFootnote 2

\xc2\xa0See, e.g., J. Wilkinson, From Brown to Bakke 11 (1979) (\xe2\x80\x9cEveryone understands that Brown v. Board of Education helped deliver the Negro from over three centuries of legal bondage\xe2\x80\x9d); Black, The Lawfulness of the Segregation Decisions, 69 Yale L.\xc2\xa0J. 421, 424\xe2\x80\x93425 (\xe2\x80\x9cHistory, too, tells us that segregation was imposed on one race by the other race; consent was not invited or required. Segregation in the South grew up and is kept going because and only because the white race has wanted it that way\xe2\x80\x94an incontrovertible fact which itself hardly consorts with equality\xe2\x80\x9d).

\nFootnote 3

\xc2\xa0I have long adhered to the view that a decision to exclude a member of a minority because of his race is fundamentally different from a decision to include a member of a minority for that reason. See, e.g., Adarand Constructors, Inc. v. Pe\xc5\x84a, 515 U. S. 200, 243, 248, n.\xc2\xa06 (1995) (Stevens, J., dissenting); Wygant v. Jackson Bd. of Ed., 476 U. S. 267, 316 (1986) (same). This distinction is critically important in the context of education. While the focus of our opinions is often on the benefits that minority schoolchildren receive from an integrated education, see, e.g., ante, at 15 (Thomas, J., concurring), children of all races benefit from integrated classrooms and playgrounds, see Wygant, 476 U. S., at 316 (\xe2\x80\x9c[T]he fact that persons of different races do, indeed, have differently colored skin, may give rise to a belief that there is some significant difference between such persons. The inclusion of minority teachers in the educational process inevitably tends to dispel that illusion whereas their exclusion could only tend to foster it\xe2\x80\x9d).

\nFootnote 4

\xc2\xa0The Chief Justice twice cites my dissent in Fullilove v. Klutznick, 448 U. S. 448 (1980). See ante, at 12, 23. In that case, I stressed the importance of confining a remedy for past wrongdoing to the members of the injured class. See 448 U. S., at 539. The present cases, unlike Fullilove but like our decision in Wygant, 476 U. S. 267, require us to \xe2\x80\x9cask whether the Board[s\xe2\x80\x99] actions[s] advanc[e] the public interest in educating children for the future,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 313 (Stevens, J., dissenting) (emphasis added). See ibid. (\xe2\x80\x9cIn my opinion, it is not necessary to find that the Board of Education has been guilty of racial discrimination in the past to support the conclusion that it has a legitimate interest in employing more black teachers in the future\xe2\x80\x9d). See also Adarand, 515 U. S., at 261\xe2\x80\x93262 (1995) (Stevens, J., dissenting) (\xe2\x80\x9cThis program, then, if in part a remedy for past discrimination, is most importantly a forward-looking response to practical problems faced by minority subcontractors\xe2\x80\x9d).

\nFootnote 5

\xc2\xa0The Chief Justice states that the Massachusetts racial imbalance Act did not require express classifications. See ante, at 31-32, n.\xc2\xa016. This is incorrect. The Massachusetts Supreme Judicial Court expressly stated:

\n

\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe racial imbalance act requires the school committee of every municipality annually to submit statistics showing the percentage of nonwhite pupils in all public schools and in each school. Whenever the board finds that racial imbalance exists in a public school, it shall give written notice to the appropriate school committee, which shall prepare a plan to eliminate imbalance and file a copy with the board. \xe2\x80\x98The term \xe2\x80\x9cracial imbalance\xe2\x80\x9d refers to a ratio between nonwhite and other students in public schools which is sharply out of balance with the racial composition of the society in which nonwhite children study, serve and work. For the purpose of this section, racial imbalance shall be deemed to exist when the per cent of nonwhite students in any public school is in excess of fifty per cent of the total number of students in such school.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d 352 Mass., at 695, 227 N.\xc2\xa0E. 2d, at 731.

\nFootnote 6

\xc2\xa0Compare ante, at 39 (\xe2\x80\x9cIt was not the inequality of the facilities but the fact of legally separating children on the basis of race on which the Court relied to find a constitutional violation in 1954\xe2\x80\x9d), with Juris. Statement in School Comm. of Boston v. Board of Education, O.\xc2\xa0T. 1967, No. 67\xe2\x80\x93759, p.\xc2\xa011 (\xe2\x80\x9cIt is implicit in Brown v. Board of Education of Topeka, 347 U. S. 483, that color or race is a constitutionally impermissible standard for the assignment of school children to public schools. We construe Brown as endorsing Mr. Justice Harlan\xe2\x80\x99s classical statement in Plessy v. Ferguson, 163 U. S. 537, 539: \xe2\x80\x98Our constitution is color-blind, and neither knows nor tolerates classes among citizens\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d).

\xc2\xa0In 1968 our mandatory jurisdiction was defined by the provision of the 1948 Judicial Code then codified at 28 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71257, see 62 Stat. 929; that provision was repealed in 1988, see 102 Stat. 662.

\nFootnote 8

\xc2\xa0For example, prior to our decision in School Comm. of Boston, the Illinois Supreme Court had issued an unpublished opinion holding unconstitutional a similar statute aimed at eliminating racial imbalance in public schools. See Juris. Statement in School Comm. of Boston v. Board of Education, O.\xc2\xa0T. 1967, No. 67\xe2\x80\x93759, at 9 (\xe2\x80\x9cUnlike the Massachusetts Court, the Illinois Supreme Court has recently held its law to eliminate racial imbalance unconstitutional on the ground that it violated the Equal Protection Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment\xe2\x80\x9d); ibid., n.\xc2\xa01. However, shortly after we dismissed the Massachusetts suit for want of a substantial federal question, the Illinois Supreme Court reversed course and upheld its statute in the published decision that Justice Breyer extensively quotes in his dissent. In so doing, the Illinois Supreme Court acted in explicit reliance on our decision in School Comm. of Boston. See 39 Ill. 2d, at 599\xe2\x80\x93600, 237 N.\xc2\xa0E. 2d, at 502 (\xe2\x80\x9cToo, the United States Supreme Court on January 15, 1968, dismissed an appeal in School Committee of Boston v. Board of Education, (Mass. 1967) 227 N.\xc2\xa0E. 2d 729, which challenged the statute providing for elimination of racial imbalance in public schools \xe2\x80\x98for want of a substantial federal question.\xe2\x80\x99 389 U.
